TRIPLE SLAYER
PAYS PENALTY
OBSlning, N. Y., Dec. 16.—Matteo
Dell 'Omo, a young Italian convicted
of triple murder In Brooklyn paid the
penalty of death in the electric chair
at Sing Sing prison this morning. His
death leaves a vacant cell in the death
house for the first time since the in
carceration there of Charles Becker
and the four gunmen. Dell 'Omo on
Nov. 13, 1911, murdered his mother,
his sister and six year old niece. His
defense was insanity.

To Leave Post Vacant. 'H
President Taft announced today
through Secretary Hilles that the post
of ambassador to Great Britain made
vacant by the death of Whitelaw Reid,
will not be filled by hipi. This an
nouncement followed a conference be
tween the president and Secretary
Knox.
Leave it to Wilson..
Several reasons were given for the
president's decision. He believes th*t
the important diplomatic questions In
volving Great Britain and the United
States can be handled successfully In
Washington by Mr. Knox and James
Bryce, the British ambassador. Ha
understands that President-elect Wil
son is also considering a man for the
London post and he does not wls)i to
---
iberation with Mr. Knox/brought a
decision to the contrary.
The post at London will not be filled
until March and it will be the
first time in many years that the
United States has been without an
ambassador In London.

Whitelaw Reid, diplomatist and
journalist, was born in Xenia, Ohio,
Oct. 27, 1837, the son of Robert Carl
ton and Marion Reid. He graduated
from Miami university, Oxford, Ohio,
receiving the degree of A. B. in 1856.

and by the University of the City of

D. in 1873, Miami the same degree in
1890, Princeton in 1899, Yale in 1901,
Cambridge in 1902, St. Andrews In
1905 and Oxford in 1907.
Shortly after his graduation from
Miami university he became editor of
the Xenia News. Duri
---
ained there until 1886. Fol

reer, engaging in the cotton business.
In 1869 he again returned to the
^newspaper field, becoming a member
of the editorial staff of the New York
Tribune and was elevated to the posi
tion of managing editor shortly there
after. Three years later he became
editor in chief and proprietor of the
same paper.
In 1881 he married the daughter of
D. Ogden Mills, a Californian of great
wealth, who removed £0 New York.
Mrs. Elizabeth Mills Reid and her
brother, Ogden Mills, divided equally
the immense estate of their father, an
estate estimated to be worth at the
time of Mr. Mills' death fully $60,000,
000. The result of the marriage was
two children, Ogden Mills Reid, who
recently succeeded his father* in the
active control of the Tribune, and Miss
Jean Reid.

United States at the coronation of
King Edward VII. In 1905 Mr. Reid
was named by President Roosevelt as
Ambassador to Great Britain.
MESSAGE FROM THE KING,
The following message was received
by President Taft from King George:
"It Is with the deepest sorrow that I
have to inform you of the death of Mr.
Whitelaw Reid at noon to-day. As
your ambassador to this country, his
loss will be sincerely deplored, while,
personally, I shall mourn for an old
friend of many .years' standing for
whom I had the greatest regard and
respect. The queen and sympathize
most warmly with Mrs. Whitelaw
Reid in her heavy sorrow."

formal public recognition of the dignity and standing
of the modern newspaper. It marked in a way that
eannot be mistaken the giant strides that Journalism
has made in the last few decades, Such a celebration

News Shocks Washington.
Washington.—News of the death in
London of Ambassador Reid came as
a shock to official Washington. It
had been generally realized that Mr.
Reid's illness was of a serious na
ture. President Taft paid an un

King George of Great Britain which
reached the White House early today.
The president also sent his sympathy
to Mrs. Reid.

Death of the Ambassador.
Whitelaw Reid died at his London reelden
re, Dorchester House, shortly after
noon yesterday from pulmonary oedema.
The end was peaceful. Mrs. Keid and
their daughter. Mrs. John Hubert Ward,
were at the bedside.

since o'clock in the morning, and at
Intervals during :he previous twenty-four
hours he had been slightly delirious as
result of the drugs administered to Induce
sleep.

Whitelaw Reid died at his London reelden
re, Dorchester House, shortly after
noon yesterday from pulmonary oedema.
The end was peaceful. Mrs. Keid and
their daughter. Mrs. John Hubert Ward,
were at the bedside.
The amiutssador had been unconscious
since o'clock in the morning, and at
Intervals during :he previous twenty-four
hours he had been slightly delirious as
result of the drugs administered to Induce
sleep.
Sir Thomas Harlow, physician to the
king, who was called in last week when
Mr. Reid's illness became acute, and his
regular physician, T>r. William Hale
White, issued the following bulletin as to
the cause of death:*
The Physician's Bulletin.
"A fortnight ago the American ambassador
had a slight bronchial attack similar
to others from which he had suffered
at considerable intervals. Wednesday last
asthma supervened, and the atshmatie
paroxysms became very severe, leading
to extreme exhaustion.
"It was hoped that he might rally, as
no pneumonic symptoms had appeared.

that Mr. Reid's Illness was of a serious
nature. Expressions of sorrow over the
loath of Ambassador Whitelaw Reid
were heard on every hand last night.

woat outside of Illinois to support Lincoln
for the presidential nomination and

work as a planter. Although
the experiment lasted only twelve
months, he was able to nearly finish his
book, entitled "Ohio in the War." which
is generally recognized as the most important
state history of the war.
Invited to Join the Tribune.
Reld's work had attracted the attention
of Horace Greeley, the most famous
newspaper man of the time, who, in 1808,
invited him to become chief e<litorial
writer or the New York Tribune, a paper
which Greeley had founded twenty-seven
years previously. Mr. Reid waj? at the
time editor-in-chief of the Cincinnati
Gazette.
When Greeley died In 1872, succumbing
practically to the great strain of lhe unsuccessful
candidature for the presidency
against Gen. Grant. Mr. Reid succeeded
him as editor and principal owner of
the Tribune.

When Greeley died In 1872, succumbing

Minister to France.
Rater in life he went into the diplomatic
service and was sent to France as United
States minister in 1889, remaining until
1892. He had twice previously declined
the post of Minister to Germany. During
that period he did some Important diplomatic
work in securing the rescinding of
the decree prohibiting the importation of
American meats into France, and also in
negotiating the reciprocity and extradition
treaties b
---
ar to year, for he Identified hiimself
with all the social, educational and other
movements of the United Kingdom. He
was chosen on many occasions as a
speaker at the inaugural ceremonies of
great publfc institutions, and was a great
favorite owing to his profound knowledge
:>f English history. He had always been
t student of English life, probably having
inherited his wish for knowledge on
this subject from his parents, who descended
from the Scottish Covenanters.
Married Miss Elizabeth Mills.
Whitelaw Reid married, in 1881, Elizabeth
Mills, daughter of the late Darius
Ogden Mills of New York. His son,
Ogden Mills Reid, is president of the
Tribune Association and managing editor
of the Trtbune. His daughter, Jean, is
the wife of the Hon. John Hubert Ward.
Mrs. Reid, in 1910, with her brother,
Ogden Mills, shared equally in a fortune
estimated at between *5u.000,000 and $W>,JOO.OOO,
which had been left them by their
father.

Thomas Valentine Purcell.
This young Ame-ican violin virtuoso
will be heard with the Schubert Co. on

WHITELAW REID
DIES IN LONDON
Ambassador a Historic Fig
ure for Many Years.
London, Dec. 15.- Whitelaw Reid,
United States ambassador to Great
Britain since 1905, died in his London
residence, Dorchester house, shorty
after noon today from pulmonary
oedema. The end was quiet and
peaceful. Mrs. Reid and their
daughter, Mrs. John Hubert Ward,
were at the bedside.
The ambassador had been uncon
scious since 9 o'clock in the morning
and at intervals in the previous 24
hours he had been slightly delirious

WHITELAW REID
DIES IN LONDON
Ambassador a Historic Fig
ure for Many Years.
London, Dec. 15.- Whitelaw Reid,
United States ambassador to Great
Britain since 1905, died in his London
residence, Dorchester house, shorty
after noon today from pulmonary
oedema. The end was quiet and
peaceful. Mrs. Reid and their
daughter, Mrs. John Hubert Ward,
were at the bedside.
The ambassador had been uncon
scious since 9 o'clock in the morning
and at intervals in the previous 24
hours he had been slightly delirious
as a result of the drugs administer
ed to induce sleep.
Sir Thomas Barlow, physician to
the king, who was called in last week
when Mr. Reid's illness became
acute, and his regular physician, Dr.
William Hale White, issued the fol

Sketch Of His Career.
Whitelaw Reid was in his seventy
sixth year. having been born Oct.
27, 1837, in Xenia, Ohio.
He became a newspaper writer as
a young man and never ended his
con ection with the public press.
He was editor-in-chief of the New
York Tribune for many years and
afterward became proprietor of that
journal. He served in the first or
ganization of the civil war in 1861
and then as war correspondent, in
which capacity he was present at
many important battles. At one
time he was librarian of the house
of representativ
---
o Great Britain
to attend the c'!lebration of the dia
mond jubilee of Queen Victoria.
His next important work was as
special commissioner to the Paris
conference which negotiated peace
between the United States and
Spain.
In 1902 he was again appointed
special ambassador to Great Britain
for the coronation of King Edward
VII.. with whom he had always been
on the most friendly terms. He
was chosen in March, 1905, United
States ambassador to Great Britain
in succession to Joseph H Choate.
In 1881 Whitelaw Reid married
Elizabeth Mills. daughter of the late
Darius Ogden Mills of New York.
His son, Ogden Mills Reid, is presi
dent of the Tribune association and
managing editor of the Tribune.
His daughter Jeanne is the wife of
the Hon. John Hubert Ward, equer
ry-in-waiting to Queen Alexandra.

correspondence both there and at
home.

WHITELAW REID,
Of New York.
The belief is general and apparent

will be one of President Harrison’s

Whitelaw Reid, editor and one of
the proprietors, of the New York Tri
bune, was born at Xenia. Ohio, in Oc
tober, 1837. His parents gave him a
good education. At fifteen he entered
the Mia ni University, at Oxford, But
ler county, Ohio, where he was grad
uated in 1856. He began the active
duties of life as principal of the graded
schools in South Charleston, Clark
county, in the same state, but did not
continue in this occupation long. In
1857 he bought the Xenia News, and
did such good work on that journal 
---
e Cincinnati
Gazette in this capacity, and in 1862
became a stockholder of that journal,
the publication of which he subse
quently assisted in the capacity of
associate editor. His connection with
the New York Tribune began with
his being the editor in charge of its
Washington bureau. He ventured
upon the publication of a volume in
the year 1865. It was entitled “After
the War—A Southern Tour,” and re
corded observations made in com
pany with Chief-justice Chase on an
extensive range of travel. Reid pub
lished another book in 1868, “Ohio in
the War,” a work of considerable
length and value. He became per
manently an editor on the staff of the
Tribune in 1870, and when Horace
Greeley was a candidate for the presi
dency, assumed the position of man
aging editor. Mr. Reid is a wealthy
man. He married the daughter of a
millionaire, and lives in style uptown
New York. The wife of the president
elect was lately entertained by him.

President Taft sent this answer to the mgaggfc of King George:
"ilis Majesty King George V, Buckingham Palace. London:
"Your majesty's sad news of the death of Mr. Whitelaw Keid has
just reached me. Mr. Reid"s death is a loss to both countries, for his
service as Ambassador was exceptional in the closer friendship that he
secured between them through his own pcrsonality. His intimate knowl
edfg ol both countries, his profound respect and love for Kngland, entirely
consistent with the highest loyalty on his part to this country,
gave him peculiar infiuence for good in his great station. I sincerely thank
your majesty for your message and your expressions of sympathy and
respett. WILLIAM H. TAFT."

President Taft also sent the following message of sympathy to Mrs.
Reid, wife of the Ambassador:
"Mrs. Whitelaw Keid, London:
"Mrs. Taft and I extend to you our deepest sympathy in your great
sorrow. The country has lost a most able and loyal public servant. Mr.
Reid's death ends one of the most notable of the careers of the great men
who have represented this nation in London. He has exercised a personal
infiuence that was exceptional in maintaining the close friendship of the
two countries. and he has been successful in a remarkable degree in the
two very heavy b

ln Th. Tiibun.
London. Dec. 16.?Whitelaw Reid, American Ambassador to
Great Britain, died at ten minutes past noon yesterday at Doichcster
House. his London residence, after an illness which began hardly
more than a fortnight ago and the serious nature of which had be?
come apparent hardly more than a few hcurs before death. The
cause of death was a bronchial attaek on which asthma supervened.
a severe asthmatic paroxysm leading to an extreme degree of ex
haustion from which he was unable to recover, though the asthma
itself was got under

 noon yesterday at Doichcster
House. his London residence, after an illness which began hardly
more than a fortnight ago and the serious nature of which had be?
come apparent hardly more than a few hcurs before death. The
cause of death was a bronchial attaek on which asthma supervened.
a severe asthmatic paroxysm leading to an extreme degree of ex
haustion from which he was unable to recover, though the asthma
itself was got under control.
With the Ambassador at the time of his death were Mrs. Reid
and their daughter, Mrs. John Ward, and the Hon. John Hubert
Ward. The physicians in attendance were Sir Thomas Barlow, Sir
William Osler, W. Hale White and Richard Rowlands.
The Ambassador had been unconscious since 9 o'clock in the
morning. and at intervals during the previous twenty-four hours he
had been slightly delirious as a result of the drugs administered to

The Ambassador returned on October 28 from his visit to Amer
ica. the whole time of which occupied only a month, his departure
from England having taken plaee on September 28. While in Amei
ica he presided at the three days' dedication exereises of the new
Edueation Building in Albany and attended to a vast amount of
business, both public and private. The result was that he reached
England considerably fatigued by the hurried trip, but instead of

unged at once into the accumulated busi?
ness of the embassy and insisted on keeping his promise to deliver

Aberstwyth, on October 31.
For the next month he was constantly einployed on the affairs
of the embassy. On December 2 he came up to Dorehester House
from Wrest Park, where he had been entertaining a shooting party
for the previous week-end, and the final illness began on the follow?
ing day. Dr. W. Hale White, Ambassador Reid's physician during
his residence in London, called Sir William Osler in consultation
two days after Mr. Reid's return from Wrest Park. Sir William
pronounced Mr. Reid in excellent condition, except for the need of
rest, and another consultation was not considered necessary until
Wednesday last, when asthma made its appearance.

candidate for the Vice-Presidency, is held Ly his
neighbors in Westchester County found expression last
evening In a friendly demonstration by the citizens

went out to Ophir Farm and serenaded Mr. Reid.
The White Plains brags band headed the serenading
party, in which there were many Democrats, among

THE TRAINING OF JOURNALISM,
From The New-York Evangelist,
The nomination of Whitelaw Reid for the Vice.
presidency 1s a distinet uibute to the power of the
press—a power which has been growing so rapidly ln
our day as to demand recognition, From the very
founding of the Government it has been in the hands
chiefly of two classes—soldlers and lawyers; the reason
for which it is easy to see. The vocation of
an Editor 1s not a speclal preparation for the duties
of a political position, and yet it Is one that ufres
ability of a high order and of a pecnilat kind. To be
the conductor of a 
---
nd the small,
which is the secret of successful administration. With no
wish to exaggerate the ability required a
pufession to which we belong, it is our candid
opinion that it requires a higher order of abllivy
to edit a metropolitan Jonrnal than to fll aa
offica in the Cabinet. Nobody, not even a soldier In
the field, needs to have his wits about him more than
an Editor. He has to act promptly, somet'mes on a
piece of news that comes In at midnight, and not to
make a mistake. This ability Mr. Reid possesses In
a remarkable degree. With his natural quickness of
mind he has been trained by long practice, first as a
reportbr In the army during the war, and since by
a quarter of a century of journalism. He Is a man
of the best character, the son of an old Covenanter, and
himself a member of the Presbyterian Church, For
that and other reasons his advent to the second place
in the Government, while It would gratify a feeling
of personal friendship, will be an honor not only to
himseif but to t

(BY THE ASSOCIATED PRESS.)
NEW YORK. Jan. 22.—Employing
all sorts of simple strategem to evade
a big corps at newspaper interview
ers, the Duke and Ducheas of Con
naught and their daughter. Princess
Patricia, tu rived here from Ottawa
today to be guests of Ambassador and
Mrs. Whitelaw Reid. The visit of
the uncle of Great Britain's ruler
marks the first call of royalty to
America -since the visit, a decade ago,
of Prince Henry of Prusela.
The visit is declared to have only
social significance.
Journey Without Incident.

was made without incident. The gov
ernor general and family were wel
comed at the Grand Central station
by Ambassador and Mrs. Retd, after
which the party motored to the home
of Mr. Reid on Madison avertue.

of Adrianople.

peoples of the English speaking race,
which he had served so well, will give
a hearty, though sad response."

Theodore Roosevelt, who appointed
the late ambassador to his post at
tendon, was absent from Sagamore
Hill during part of the day and had
retired when news of Mr. Reid's death
reached him. Through a member of
the family he expressed his deepest
concern at the tidings which he said
was a severe blow to him personally,
as he had known Mr. Reid Intimately
for years.

and In this set a noble example for

London, Dec. 16. —Whitelaw Reid,
United States ambassador to Great
Britain since 1905, died Sunday after

residence, of pulmonary oedema. Mr.

cabled Ogden to sail immediately for
England.
Ambassador Reid’s breakdown dates
from his trip to New York last winter
to entertain the duke and duchess of
Connaught, who came from Ottawa to
be the ambassador's guests at his
town house in New York.

Whitelaw Reid was born near Xenia,
0., Oct. 27, 1837. The town was found
ed by his paternal grandfather, a Scot
tish Covenanter, and his mother was
Marian Whitelaw Ronalds of ancient
Scottish descent. His early education
was received from the teaching of the
Rev. Hugh McMillan, an uncle, who
was principal of the academy of
Xenia and one of the trustees of
Miami university. Mr. Reid was grad
uated with class honors from Miami
in 1856.
At the age of 20 years he took up
journalism. He became editor and pro
prietor of the Xenia News, and speed
ily made his power felt as a strong
factor in the Republican party. He
chose Horace Greeley as his guide in
newspaper work and modeled his
sheet after the New York Tribune.
In 1.881 he married the daughter of
D. O. Mills. During his early married
life he published several books on
journalism and politics.

Whitelaw Reid, diplomatisf ®nd
Journalist, was born in Xenia, Ohio,
Oct. 27, 1837, the sou of Robert Carl
ton and Marion Reid. He
from Miami university, Oxford Ohio,
receiving the degree of A. B. IJI^6B.
The degree of A. M. was

Shortly after his graduation from

the Xenia News. During his connec
tion with this paper he acted In the
capacity of legis
---
ained there until 1886. Fol
lowing this he adopted a business car
reer, engaging in the cotton business.
In 1869 be again returned to the
newspaper field, becoming a member
of the editorial staff of the New York
Tribune and was elevated to the posi
tion of managing editor shortly there
after. Three years later he became
editor in chief and proprietor of the
same paper.
In 1881 he married the daughter of
D. Ogden Mills, a Californian of great
wealth, who removed to New York.
Mrs. Elizabeth Mills Reid and her
brother, Ogden Mills, divided equally
the immense estate of their father, an
estate estimated to be worth at the
time of Mr. Mills’ death fully $60,000,-
000. The result of the marriage was
two children, Ogden Mills Reid, who
recently succeeded his father in the
active control of the Tribune, and Miss
Jean Reid.
In 1877 and again in 1881 Mr.
Reid was appointed United States
minister to Germany. He was elected
to the board of regents of the State
University of New York. In 1902 he

TAFT WILL NAME
SUCCESSOR SOON
Several Names Alieady Men
tioned For St. James Court.
Washington, Dec. 26.—The Ameri
can ambassadorship at London will
not long be left vacant.
Although President Taft was said
not to have decided at this time upon
a successor to Mr. Reid, it is under
stood that he will fill the place within
a few weeks. The president regards
the diplomatic problems in which this
country and Great Britain are at pres
ent entangled as too important to
leave the United States unrepresented
even for a few months at the court of
St. James.
Within the next few days and prob
ably during his trip to Panama the
president will scan carefully names
of men he considers available for the
post, and a nomination may be sent to
the senate early in the year.
Th
---
he
post, and a nomination may be sent to
the senate early in the year.
The president is understood to be
hopeful that the senat will confirm
any nomination for thi, ambassador
ship which he sends in. It is the cus
tom for diplomats holding important
posts to resign with a change of ad
ministration, and therefore the presi
dent believes that the Democrats
would have no obpect In holding up
be for only tT Tew months! 1
Among the names that have been
mentioned as a possible cussessor to
Ambassador Reid have been those of
former Vice President Charles W.
Fairbanks and John Hays Hammond,
while other well known Republicans
of wealth, among them Edward T.
Stotesbury, of Philadelphia, have been
talked about for the London post in
circles close to the president.

GOING ABROAD.
Emperor of Japan Will Visit Europe
In Japanese Warship.
Toklo, Jan. 4.—The Emperor of
Japan, after his coronation next No
vember, is planning an European trip
aboard a Japanese warship, according
to local newspapers.

PRESIDENT ATTENDS.
Arrives at New York to Be Present at
Funeral of Ambassador.
New York, Jan. 4. —President Taft
arrived here this morning to attend
the funeral of Whitelaw Reid at the
Episcopal Cathedral of St. John the
Divine. This afternoon he will ad
dress the international peace forum
and tonight he will be the guest at a
dinner of the republican organizations
of New York, Philadelphia and Boston.
He will return to Washington to
morrow.

band furnished the music,
Harvest is over and some are thresh

a few days ago at the home of her zon
in-law, Robert Williams.
The Normal School at Green ill is

or thirty scholars will be in attendance,
Messrs. Bowman and Hottel will be the

degrees, daily, below the normal for this

Jones—November 15th, 1899, at
Berryville, Va Mrs. Sun Neill Jones,
aged 25 years. She was the wife of
J Terrell Jones and the daughter of
Isaac Bowman, of Berryville. Giv
ing her heart to Christ when a girl,
as the years passed over her head,
she g-ew more and more useful in
the Master’s service. Her church
was her pride, the object for which
she constantly labored and prayed.

derful power, and as leader of the
choir she led the praises of the sanc
tuary. In every department of the
church's work she was always ready
to lend a helping land. While she
hovered between life and death earn

Arsenical poisoning, as well as
hives, is being brought about by
spring strawberries. A number
of cases have been called to the
attention of prominent physicians
in different parts of California
and Nevada, whose diagnoses
cannot be disputed. According
to the prevailing belief, these
cases are brought about by the
strawberry growers putting ar
senic on the berries in order to
keep away the bugs which are
liable to destroy them.
It is with much regret that we
learn of the death of Isaac H.
Bowman of Ketchum. On June
4th he received a stroke of par
alysis and died the same evening.
Mr. Bowman was one of Idaho's
early pioneers, and has many
friends on Salmon river. For
years he was editor of the Ketch
um Keystone, and was consider
ed one of the ablest writers in
Idaho. He was an open-hearted
man, and was his own worst en
emy, but was always on the side
of humanity and justice.

Their broken fragments to the sunset sailing.
Its radiance cheers the coming heirs of time,
The men and women of the near hereafter.
And in the glory of its light sublime
Are lost the world’s derisive scorn and laughter.
O, pioneer and prophet, years fourscore
To rob your soul of youth have lost their power
Who lives a life for others holds in store
A charm defying age or fleeting hour.
Boston, Nov• 12.1805.

until a Girl's Department was established

the gates of learning and the world was
still tinctured with the malice which
stoned Mile. Lambroge for daring to

•Roberts Brothers, Boston. Price, $1 00.
tinued for six winters, taking up various
literary and ethical subjects. Margaret’s
method was to begin each session with a
short introduction of the subject suggest
ing the most effective points ot view.
She then invited questions or criticisms
and if these were not forthcoming she
elicited thought by skillful questioning.
Elizabeth P. Peabody said there was
never anything like these classes. Mar
garet seemed to have absolute insight
into everything.
Margaret Fuller was the organizer and
controlling spirit of the first thoroughly
American literary enterprise the Dial
founded in 1840 which was the organ of
that remarkable spiritual transfiguration
known as “Transcendentalism.” In 1843
the Dial passed under the management
of Ralph Waldo Emerson as Miss
Fuller could no longer afford to give her
services for the munificent sum of $200
a year. She still gave to it much of its

Margaret Fuller was the organizer and
controlling spirit of the first thoroughly
American literary enterprise the Dial
founded in 1840 which was the organ of
that remarkable spiritual transfiguration
known as “Transcendentalism.” In 1843
the Dial passed under the management
of Ralph Waldo Emerson as Miss
Fuller could no longer afford to give her
services for the munificent sum of $200
a year. She still gave to it much of its
best material, among tliis the paper en
titled “The Great Lawsuit.—Man versus
Men; Woman versus Women.” This
was afterwards enlarged into what is
considered her greatest production,
“Woman in the Nineteenth Century.” j
It contains the gist ot all that has been
written or spoken on the broad question
of the rights of woman. She showed
that man could only have his own free
dom by ma
---
at she might inves
tigate the philanthropic questions of the

mers, Lydia Maria Child and William
Henry Channing who were engaged in
this work for the New York Tribune.
She wrote about art, the drama, literary,
lectures and about three articles a week
on the work of various reform and ben
evolent associations which as Mr. Gree
ley said never received such wise dis
criminating commendation to the favor
of the rich as they did from her pen.
The tragic death of Margaret Fuller
ensured for her story to all time the
keenest interest in all who learn it. To
her friends her memory became a sacred
possession, and the record of her deeds
and of her worthy aims became a part of
true American history.

WOMEN IN INDIA.
There are two magazines for women
in Madras conducted entirely by Indian
ladies, one in English, called the Indian
Ladies’ Journal, and the other in Te
lugu.
In Bengal there are also two, but both
are conducted in Bengali by Brahma
ladies.
In the Punjab the Arya Samaj has
taken the lead in the education of girls
along Hindu lines, and there is a govern

Margaret Fuller was born of New Eng
land folk May 23, 1810. Her father was
Hon. Timothy Fuller, a lawyer who
served several terms in the Massachu

Margaret Crane, was amiable and self

moved to the eight different homes they

When the biographies of Margaret Ful
ler were written nothing was known of
the love episode which was first made
known to the public when a book was
published in 1903 entitled “Love Letters
of Margaret Fuller.” Her correspondent
was James Nathan, a German, born in
1811 and who lived in America from 1830
to 1862. He died in Hamburg, Germany
in 1888. It was in 1873 that Mr. Na
than for the first time gave these letters
to a friend that they might some time
be published, which was not done until
thirty years after.
Apologizing for parting with what he
called the offspring of their spiritual in
tercourse, he said: “I cannot suffer
their exquisite naturalness and sweet
ness to sink into the grave. More e
---
 from others, who, having deeply
loved her in life and mourned her death,
are entitled to this sacred experience of
her inmost soul, while at the same time I
feel I can wreathe no fresher laurels
around the cherished memory of ‘Margar
et’ than by showing, through these let
ters, that great and gifted as she was as
a writer, she was no less so in the soft
and tender emotions of a true woman's
heart.”

THORNE.
Lillian Whiting, writing from Boston
to the New Orleans Times-Democrat,
says: Mr. Julian Hawthorne has sown
the dragon’s teeth in his publication of a
paragraph regarding Margaret Fuller,
from the private diary of his father, in the
mew books entitled Nathaniel Haw*
thorne and his wife.” The post mortem
possibilities of authors’ manuscripts add
a new terror to death, and one would im
agine all literary people might follow the
example ol the poet Whittier, who im
mediately burnt all his letters after read
ing the revelations of the Carlyle house
hold. The impression made by Mr.
Froude’s indiscretion has been a deep
and a painful one, and it has moved Mr.
Frederic Harrison to mor
---
r not have heard. Those who
loved him, and those who loved her, have
been shocked, amazed, ashamed, in
turn.”
The revelations of the Hawthorne
home have offered us nothing of this
painful character. It was an ideally
beautiful marriage, an ideally happy
home. If theie was poverty of the purse
there was no poverty of the spirit. But
Mr. Julian Hawthorne’s work presented
its vulnerable point in the extract to which
I refer from his father’s diary in which
the elder Hawthorne had said of Mar
garet Fuller that she had a strong and
J coarse naturethat she had not left in
the hearts and minds of those who knew
her any deep witness of her integrity and
purity. She was a great humbug—of
course with much talent and much moral
reality, or she could never have been so
great a humbug. She had
stock herselt full of borrowed qualities
which she chose to provide herself with,
There appears to have been a total col
lapse in poor Margaret, morally and in
lectually and, tragic as her catastrophe
was, Providen
---

their child on beard that ill-fated ship.
She proved herself a very
woman after all, and fell, as the weakest
of her sisters might.”
The books were published and read,
and the paragraph trom which I make
this quotation was noted with surprise,
and with that shock and sense of spiritual
loss with which we receive the first revela
tion that our idols are only clay after all.
But it vr-', not without reference to Mar
garet Fuller that this shock came, as it
was with reference to Hawthorne him
self. The character of Miss Fuller, both
personally and in literature, is almost
curiously secure, when one realizes how
limited in quantity is her literary work.
It is, too, work that is revealed only to
the reader who is by temperamentality

sight, her sympathetic comprehension of

Born, May 33, 1810; Died, July 19, I860.
Margaret the forty years since o’er thy head
Relentless rolled the surges of the deep
Saw souls and nations starting out of sleep,
And fettered freedom rising from the dead
In Rome whose blood beside thy tears was
Couldst thou come back today, thy heart
would leap
To see what thou hast sown the thousands
While women’s feet press on where thou
hast led.
And dreams that shone before thy proph

rolled.

Gleam out of sorrow’s blackness line on
To thousand souls in sadness seeking
thine
For courage clear, and comfort manirola.
Thy faith in God, in self, in truth was
Thou woman’s pioneer in Freedom s
Thy work'shall we “not wilingly let die”;
Thy name, like banner high oer. battle
Lives like that faith once grandly lived by
thee,
A watchword and a pledge of victory.

was written forThe Woman’s Tribune in
1890 by Miss Emily Tilghman, a gifted
and learned lady of Philadelphia, who
witote much for The Woman’s Tribune
for a number of years under the pen
name given here. This poem was lost
and is only just found, unfortunately not
in time to be given in connection with
The Tribune’s story of Margaret Fuller,
which has been running in the four pre*
vious issues, but it should he put with
them by those who are saving them for
reference.

Berlin, Vienna and London. She is a
woman who is bound to be in the van of
her profession.

meeting in the early days of June._

en as a consolation prize

lect: I am life,’’ railed Mar

Miss Chipperfleld's study is
passionately It is

timental, partaking often of

has lived, not so much as a

feminine. Hear her on the
subject of men:
“Men disappoint me so.
The best of you are so unwise,
the wisest are so ignoble, the
truest are so cold. I wish I
were a man and then there
would be one.”

Byrneses Entertain Their Niece;
Other Parties for Younger Set
By Betty Milliken
The former Secretary of State and Mrs. James F. Byrnes Altered
the limelight yesterday—not in an official capacity but In a purely social
one. Mr. Byrnes’ great-niece, Margaret Fuller, arrived the night before
to spend her vacation from Newton College with the Byrneses. Her aunt,
Mrs. Worthington Miner, met her in New York and came with her to
Washington. The latter is well known here, first because she is a native
Washingtonian who lived here until her marriage; and second, because
she has made a name for herself on the legitimate stage, using her maiden
name, Frances Fuller.
So for the first time since Mr. Byrnes gave up his duties at the State
Department, he and his graci
---
 yesterday—not in an official capacity but In a purely social
one. Mr. Byrnes’ great-niece, Margaret Fuller, arrived the night before
to spend her vacation from Newton College with the Byrneses. Her aunt,
Mrs. Worthington Miner, met her in New York and came with her to
Washington. The latter is well known here, first because she is a native
Washingtonian who lived here until her marriage; and second, because
she has made a name for herself on the legitimate stage, using her maiden
name, Frances Fuller.
So for the first time since Mr. Byrnes gave up his duties at the State
Department, he and his gracious wife assumed the role of hosts at a
large party yesterday. It was mostly a party for young people, a tea-dance
given for their young niece at the Congressional Club.
Palms and ferns with red poinsettias and white carnations decorated
the clubhouse for the gay occasion and the young set danced to the tune
ful music of David McWilliams''
orchestra.
The attractive brunette guest of
honor wore a

Mr. Norman G. Cohen, son of Mr.
and Mrs. Aaron Cohen of Wash
ington and Atlantic City. The
wedding is planned for early April.
Miss Broner was graduated from
Boston University with the degree
of bachelor of science in journalism.
Mr. Cohen, a veteran of two years’
service in the Army, was graduated
from the Wharton School at the
University of Pennsylvania with a
degree of bachelor of science In
economics.
MISS MARGARET FULLER.
—Hessler Photo.

Keywords: bank, bonds, died, investment

-five
years ago—Ralph Waldo Emerson,
American philosopher, poet, essayist
and lecturer, died at Concord, Mass.
Graduated from Harvard, where he
was class poet, Emerson taught school
at Boston and then attended Harvard
Divinity School. He became pastor of
the Old North Church in Boston in
1828 but resigned from his church and
the ministry itself five years later. R-e
turning to America from a tour of
Europe, he settled at Concord in 1835,
where he formed friendships with
Thoreau, Bronson Alcott, Margaret
Fuller, and Nathaniel Hawthorne. His
first philosophical publication, ‘Nature’
was a fundamental exposition of tran
scendentalism, a movement which
flourished in New England in Emer
son's day, and which developed a
mystical belief in individualism and
self-reliance. The quarter century after
1840 constituted the period of his
greatest activity as a lecturer. His Es
says established his reputation in the
United States and Europe. Active in
the anti-slavery movement, Emerson
wrote The Conduct of Life (1

To Highlight
Youth Council Meet
The first meeting in 1947 of
the Savannah Youth Council,
NAACP, will be held on Monday
evening, January 6, at 6 o’clock
in the West Broad Street Y.M.C.A.
and it promises to be a meeting
not to be forgotten. Plans have
been made to have the music, for
this occasion, furnished by the
female quintette headed by Miss
Margaret Fuller and the Senti
mental Gentlemen, both, are
among the finest musical groups,
in the city.
Two movies, entitled “On Guard”
and “Team Work” will be shown.
Rubin Brown has kindly con
sented to serve as operator of
the motion picture projector.
The highlight of the evening
will be an address by Matthew
S. Brown, the chairman of Vet
erans’ Affairs. He is a dynamic
speaker and a militant youth lead
er, his speech will be informative
and encouraging.

eps in that application of the higher
critical and literary culture to the uses of the daily

never succeeded in wholly subduing her moods to
the exactions of a regular periodical task, shaped

tical adaptability as well as intellectual force. How
far Margaret Fuller's essays in newspaper criticism
contributed to the advancement of the literary
movement in which she was concerned can never be
measured. That they bad a powerful effect may be
taken for granted but cannot be proved. Certainly

many vague teudencies, and expression to a great
many vague wishes, and they must have rouscd
many weak spirits, besides promoting the serious
culture of which the American press forty years ago
